Al-Amidi's Objection on Time as Part of the Object
Al-Amidi objects that time is part of the object; the author clarifies the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's objection that time is part of the object of knowledge, and the author's clarification that when the object's time differs, the object itself becomes multiple.

Core Idea
Time can be part of the object, making the object multiple when time changes.

This passage presents a nuanced objection by al-Amidi to the majority view on similarity of knowledges. He argues that when time is part of the object, different times make the objects different, so the knowledges are not similar. The author then clarifies the correct view, distinguishing cases where time is external versus internal to the object. Let's explore this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the objection and the author's response.
2
Identify al-Amidi's objection
Find how al-Amidi argues that time is included in the object. Consider his example of Zayd's standing now versus tomorrow.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author distinguishes between the object being multiple when time is part of it and the object being single when time is external. Grasp this key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض الآمدى بأن الفرق ظاهر فإن الوقت هاهنا داخل في متعلق العلم كالعلم بقيام زيد الآن وقيامه غداً، ولا خفاء في اختلاف الكل باختلاف الجزء بخلاف كون الجوهر في زمانين فإنه خارج عنه، وإنما نظير ذلك العلم بالشيء في وقتين لا العلم بمعلوم مقيد بوقتين، هذا والحق أن المعلوم إذا اختلف وقته كان متعدداً لا متحداً وأن اتحاده مع تعدد العلم إنما يتصور عند اختلاف وقت العلم، والظاهر أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو عند اختلاف محله وقد سبق الكلام في أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو مختلفان.
Al-Amidi objected that the difference is apparent, for time here is included in the object of knowledge, like knowledge of Zayd's standing now and his standing tomorrow, and there is no hiddenness in the difference of the whole by difference of the part, unlike the case of a substance being in two times, for it is outside it; rather the analogy for that is knowledge of a thing in two times, not knowledge of an object restricted by two times. This, and the truth is that when the time of the object differs, it is multiple not single, and its unity with multiplicity of knowledge is only conceivable when the time of the knowledge differs, and the apparent is that they are then similar, or when its locus differs, and there has preceded speech about whether they are then similar or different.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Amidi and the author's response. Al-Amidi objects to the majority view that two knowledges with the same object at different times are similar. He argues that the difference is clear because time here is part of the object of knowledge (متعلق العلم). For example, knowledge of 'Zayd standing now' and knowledge of 'Zayd standing tomorrow' have different objects because the time is part of the known state. Thus, the whole (the knowledge) differs when a part (time) differs. This is unlike the case of a substance (جوهر) existing in two times, where time is external to the substance. The proper analogy would be knowledge of a thing in two times (without time being part of the object) not knowledge of an object restricted by two times. The author then gives the correct view (الحق): when the time of the object differs, the object is multiple, not one. So the earlier case where the object is one is only when the time of the object is the same. Unity of the object with multiple knowledges is conceivable only when the time of the knowledge differs (i.e., two knowledges occur at different times of the same object) or when the subject (محل) differs. The author notes that the case of different subjects was discussed earlier, and it was debated whether the knowledges are similar or different.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that time difference always makes two knowledges different even when the object is the same.
Correction: The author clarifies that if time is part of the object, the object becomes multiple, so the knowledges are of different objects. But if time is external (as in a substance), the object can remain single.

Checkpoint

A) Two knowledges cannot have the same object
B) Time is part of the object, so different times make the objects different
C) Majority view is based on a misunderstanding of substances
D) Objects cannot be restricted by time

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's objection shows that when time is part of the object, differing times make the object multiple, so the majority view stands only when time is not part of the object.

The Known Object and Its Time: Why Change Means Multiplicity
The author argues that a known object whose time differs is multiple, not one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a known object must be considered multiple if its time differs, according to the author's argument.

Core Idea
A known object is tied to its time; change the time, and you change the object.

The author grapples with a subtle problem: can the same thing be known at two different times and still be one? Common sense might say yes, but philosophical rigor demands a closer look. This passage dismantles that intuition by distinguishing between the external existence of a substance and our knowledge of it, and argues that the known object itself becomes multiple when its time differs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'existence in two times' and 'knowledge in two times'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how the author distinguishes between the substance existing in two times and knowledge of a thing in two times. The part is unlike the substance's existence.
This sets the stage for the argument.
3
Locate the core thesis
Find the sentence stating: 'if the time of the known object differs, it becomes multiple, not one.' This is the central claim.
Identify where this sentence begins.
4
Consider the implications for multiple instances of knowledge
Understand that oneness of the object with multiple instances of knowledge is only possible if the times of knowledge differ. This clarifies the relationship between knowledge and its object.
The author discusses when two instances of knowledge are similar or different.
5
Connect to the previous discussion
Note the reference to an earlier discussion about similarity vs. difference of two instances of knowledge. This passage builds on that foundation.
This shows continuity in the author's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزء بخلاف كون الجوهر في زمانين فإنه خارج عنه، وإنما نظير ذلك العلم بالشيء في وقتين لا العلم بمعلوم مقيد بوقتين، هذا والحق أن المعلوم إذا اختلف وقته كان متعدداً لا متحداً وأن اتحاده مع تعدد العلم إنما يتصور عند اختلاف وقت العلم، والظاهر أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو عند اختلاف محله وقد سبق الكلام في أنهما حينئذ مثلان أو مختلفان.
The part is unlike the existence of the substance in two times, for it is outside of it. Rather, the analogy for that is knowledge of a thing in two times, not knowledge of a known object restricted to two times. This, and the truth is that if the time of the known object differs, it becomes multiple, not one, and that its oneness with multiplicity of knowledge is only conceivable when the time of knowledge differs. And the apparent is that they (i.e., the two instances of knowledge) are then either similar or [different] due to differing locus, and the discussion has preceded regarding whether they are then similar or different.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the relationship between the known object (al-ma'lum) and time. The author distinguishes between the external existence of a substance in two times and the case of knowledge. He argues that if the time of the known object changes, the object itself becomes multiple, not one. The oneness of an object with multiple instances of knowledge is only conceivable when the instants of knowledge themselves differ, not the object's time. The apparent conclusion is that two instances of knowledge of the same object at different times are either similar or different, depending on the locus (mahall) of knowledge. The author refers to a previous discussion on whether they are similar or different.

The Heart as the Seat of Knowledge
Author argues heart (spirit) is locus of knowledge via scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the heart, interpreted as the spirit (ruh), is the locus of originated knowledge according to scriptural evidence, and the distinction from the physical heart and the philosophical rational soul.

Core Idea
The heart, as the spirit, is the seat of knowledge—a key theological claim based on revelation.

In this investigation, the author establishes that acquired knowledge resides in the heart, but clarifies that 'heart' refers to the spirit (ruh) that distinguishes humans, not the physical organ. This distinction is crucial for understanding how human knowledge relates to divine creation and sets the stage for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and its support
Read the passage and note the author's assertion: the locus of knowledge is the heart, supported by scriptural proof (sam'). Observe that he acknowledges reason alone does not necessitate this, but revelation indicates it.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author stresses that 'heart' here does not mean the physical organ common to animals, but the spirit (ruh) that distinguishes humans. This is the view of verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun).
The philosophers identify it as the rational soul (nafs natiqa), but the author notes it uses instruments for particulars.
3
Reflect on the philosophical view
Consider the philosophers' position that the locus is the rational soul, and how the author's view differs. This will be expanded later.
This prepares for a deeper discussion on the nature of the soul and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس: محل العلم هو القلب (۱) بدليل السمع، وإن جاز أن يخلقه الله تعالى في أى جوهر شاء، إلا أن الظاهر أن ليس المراد بالقلب هو ذلك العضو، وعند الفلاسفة هو النفس الناطقة، إلا انه في الجزئيات يتوسط الآلات، وسيجيء لهذا زيادة بيان. قد دلت الأدلة السمعية من الكتاب والسنة على أن محل العلم الحادث هو القلب، وإن لم يتعين هو لذلك عقلا، بل يجوز أن يخلقه الله تعالى في أى جوهر شاء، لكن الظاهر من كلام كثير من المحققين أن ليس المراد بالقلب ذلك العضو المخصوص الموجود لجميع الحيوانات، بل الروح (۲) الذي به امتياز الإنسان. (۱) القلب: الفؤاد وقد يعبر به عن العقل وقال الفراء القلب العقل. وقيل القلب أخص من الفؤاد ومنه الحديث أتاكم أهل اليمن أرق قلوباً وألين أفئدة. وقد ورد القلب في القرآن على ثلاثة معان. الأول: بمعنى العقل: إن في ذلك لذكرى لمن كان له قلب. الثاني: بمعنى الرأي والتدبير: وقلوبهم شتى أي آراؤهم مختلفة. الثالث: بمعنى حقيقة القلب الذي في الصدر: ولكن تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور. سورة الحج آية رقم ٤٦. (۲) الروح: اسماً للنفس، وذلك لكون للنفس بعض الروح. فهو كتسمية النوع باسم الجنس. نحو تسمية الإنسان بالحيوان. والروح في القرآن على سبعة أوجه: الأول بمعنى الرحمة: وأيدهم بروح منه أي رحمة، والثاني بمعنى الملك: يوم يقوم الروح والملائكة صفا، الثالث: -
The Sixth Investigation: The locus of knowledge is the heart, by scriptural proof, even though it is permissible for Allah to create it in any substance He wills, except that the apparent meaning is that by 'heart' is not meant that (physical) organ. According to the philosophers, it is the rational soul, except that in particulars it mediates through instruments, and further explanation of this will come. Indeed, the scriptural evidences from the Book and the Sunnah have indicated that the locus of originated knowledge is the heart, even if it is not determined for that by reason, rather it is permissible for Allah to create it in any substance He wills. But the apparent from the speech of many verifying scholars is that by 'heart' is not meant that specific organ found in all animals, but rather the spirit (ruh) by which man is distinguished. (1) Al-qalb: the heart (fu'ad), and it may be used to mean the intellect (al-'aql). Al-Farra' said: al-qalb is al-'aql. It is said that al-qalb is more specific than al-fu'ad, and from it is the hadith: 'The people of Yemen have come to you; they are the softest of hearts (qulub) and the most tender of feelings (af'ida).' And the word qalb has occurred in the Qur'an with three meanings: First: meaning intellect (al-'aql): 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' (Q 50:37). Second: meaning opinion and management: 'Their hearts are diverse' meaning their opinions are different. Third: meaning the actual heart which is in the chest: 'But blind are the hearts that are in the chests' (Surah al-Hajj 22:46). (2) Al-ruh: a name for the soul (al-nafs), because the soul has some of the spirit. It is like naming a species by the genus, such as naming a human by 'animal'. And al-ruh in the Qur'an has seven aspects: first: meaning mercy: 'He supported them with a spirit from Him' i.e., mercy; second: meaning the angel: 'On the day when the spirit and the angels stand in rows'; third: -
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is the sixth investigation (al-mabhas al-sadis) of the work, examining the locus of acquired knowledge (al-ilm al-hadith). The author states the position: the locus is the heart (al-qalb), supported by scriptural evidence (sam'iyyat), though rationally Allah could place it in any substance. He notes that philosophers identify it as the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa) which uses bodily instruments for particulars. Then the author clarifies that 'heart' here does not mean the physical organ but rather the spirit (al-ruh) that distinguishes humans, as affirmed by many verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun). The passage then provides lexical explanations: for 'qalb', it lists three Quranic meanings—intellect, opinion, and the physical heart; for 'ruh', it begins to list seven meanings but only gives the first two (mercy and angel) before the text cuts off. These clarifications prevent misunderstanding that the locus of knowledge is the physical heart.

نفس ناطقة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the heart mentioned as the locus of knowledge is the physical heart organ.
Correction: The author explains that the word 'heart' here means the spirit (ruh), not the physical organ common to all animals.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the actual locus of acquired knowledge?
A) The physical heart
B) The rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa)
C) The spirit (al-ruh)
D) The brain

Key Takeaway
The locus of acquired knowledge is the spirit (ruh), not the physical heart, as indicated by scriptural evidence.

The Qur'anic Meanings of Qalb and Ruh
Author lists lexical meanings of 'qalb' and 'ruh' in the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Qur'anic meanings of 'qalb' (heart) and the first two of seven meanings of 'ruh' (spirit) as explained by the author.

Core Idea
The words 'qalb' and 'ruh' in the Qur'an have multiple meanings; the author lists them to prevent misinterpretation.

After establishing that the locus of knowledge is the heart (spirit), the author provides lexical clarifications of the key terms 'qalb' and 'ruh' as they appear in the Qur'an. These distinctions are essential for correctly interpreting theological texts and avoiding confusion between literal and metaphorical uses.

Guided Path
1
Examine the three meanings of qalb
Read the list of three meanings: intellect (Q 50:37), opinion/management, and the physical heart (Q 22:46). Note the Qur'anic proof for each and how they differ.
The first meaning (intellect) is relevant to the earlier discussion of the locus of knowledge.
2
Begin the meanings of ruh
Read the first two meanings of ruh: mercy (Q 58:22) and angel (Q 78:38). The text is truncated, but understand that ruh has multiple senses.
The author intended to list seven meanings, but only two are preserved due to the cut-off text.

The Sixth Investigation: The locus of knowledge is the heart, by scriptural proof, even though it is permissible for Allah to create it in any substance He wills, except that the apparent meaning is that by 'heart' is not meant that (physical) organ. According to the philosophers, it is the rational soul, except that in particulars it mediates through instruments, and further explanation of this will come. Indeed, the scriptural evidences from the Book and the Sunnah have indicated that the locus of originated knowledge is the heart, even if it is not determined for that by reason, rather it is permissible for Allah to create it in any substance He wills. But the apparent from the speech of many verifying scholars is that by 'heart' is not meant that specific organ found in all animals, but rather the spirit (ruh) by which man is distinguished. (1) Al-qalb: the heart (fu'ad), and it may be used to mean the intellect (al-'aql). Al-Farra' said: al-qalb is al-'aql. It is said that al-qalb is more specific than al-fu'ad, and from it is the hadith: 'The people of Yemen have come to you; they are the softest of hearts (qulub) and the most tender of feelings (af'ida).' And the word qalb has occurred in the Qur'an with three meanings: First: meaning intellect (al-'aql): 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' (Q 50:37). Second: meaning opinion and management: 'Their hearts are diverse' meaning their opinions are different. Third: meaning the actual heart which is in the chest: 'But blind are the hearts that are in the chests' (Surah al-Hajj 22:46). (2) Al-ruh: a name for the soul (al-nafs), because the soul has some of the spirit. It is like naming a species by the genus, such as naming a human by 'animal'. And al-ruh in the Qur'an has seven aspects: first: meaning mercy: 'He supported them with a spirit from Him' i.e., mercy; second: meaning the angel: 'On the day when the spirit and the angels stand in rows'; third: -
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is the sixth investigation (al-mabhas al-sadis) of the work, examining the locus of acquired knowledge (al-ilm al-hadith). The author states the position: the locus is the heart (al-qalb), supported by scriptural evidence (sam'iyyat), though rationally Allah could place it in any substance. He notes that philosophers identify it as the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa) which uses bodily instruments for particulars. Then the author clarifies that 'heart' here does not mean the physical organ but rather the spirit (al-ruh) that distinguishes humans, as affirmed by many verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun). The passage then provides lexical explanations: for 'qalb', it lists three Quranic meanings—intellect, opinion, and the physical heart; for 'ruh', it begins to list seven meanings but only gives the first two (mercy and angel) before the text cuts off. These clarifications prevent misunderstanding that the locus of knowledge is the physical heart.

The Locus of Originated Knowledge: Heart or Soul?
Defines locus of knowledge as heart (spirit) with transmitted proof and philosophical contrast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological position that the locus of originated knowledge is the heart (meaning the spirit), and how it differs from the philosophical view of the rational soul.

Core Idea
Knowledge resides in the heart—not the physical organ, but the spirit that distinguishes humans.

This passage from al-Maqasid tackles a fundamental question: where does knowledge take place? The author asserts that transmitted revelation points to the heart as the seat of knowledge, but immediately clarifies that 'heart' here does not mean the physical organ. He then contrasts this with the philosophers' view that the soul is the locus, with instruments for particulars. This lesson unpacks these positions, grounding the doctrine in transmitted evidence while acknowledging rational possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Notice the author's structured presentation: statement, transmitted evidence, clarification, then philosophical contrast.
The passage uses key terms like 'قلب' (heart) and 'نفس ناطقة' (rational soul).
2
Identify the author's main claim and its evidence
Find where the author states that the locus of originated knowledge is the heart, and what transmitted evidence (السمع) he refers to. The passage mentions 'دلت الأدلة السمعية'—the Book and Sunnah.
The author also notes rational possibility: God could create knowledge in any substance.
3
Understand the clarification: heart ≠ physical organ
Notice the explicit denial that 'heart' means the physical organ common to all animals. Instead, it refers to 'الروح' (the spirit) that distinguishes humans. This avoids a crude materialist interpretation.
This clarification is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
4
Compare with the philosophers' view
The philosophers say the locus of universals is the rational soul, and of particulars is the senses. But the investigators among them say the soul is the locus for all, with instruments for particulars. Contrast this with the author's heart/spirit view.
The author promises more detail later on the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس : محل العلم هو القلب (۱) بدليل السمع، وإن جاز أن يخلقه الله تعالى في أى جوهر شاء، إلا أن الظاهر أن ليس المراد بالقلب هو ذلك العضو، وعند الفلاسفة هو النفس الناطقة ، إلا انه فى الجزئيات يتوسط الآلات، وسيجيء لهذا زيادة (بيان) .
قد دلت الأدلة السمعية من الكتاب والسنة على أن محل العلم الحادث هو القلب، وإن لم يتعين هو لذلك عقلا، بل يجوز أن يخلقه الله تعالى في أى جوهر شاء، لكن الظاهر من كلام كثير من المحققين أن ليس المراد بالقلب ذلك العضو المخصوص الموجود لجميع الحيوانات، بل الروح (۲) الذي به امتياز الإنسان .
وظاهر كلام الفلاسفة أن محل العلم بالكليات هو النفس الناطقة المجردة، وبالجزئيات هو المشاعر الظاهرة أو الباطنة، إلا أن المحققين منهم على أن محل الكل هو النفس، إلا أنه في الكليات يكون بالذات، وفي الجزئيات بتوسط الآلات أعنى المشاعر، وسيجيء بيان ذلك في بحث النفس.
The Sixth Discussion: The locus of knowledge is the heart, by evidence of transmitted revelation, although it is permissible that God Almighty may create it in any substance He wills, except that the apparent meaning is that what is meant by 'heart' is not that organ. According to the philosophers, it is the rational soul, except that in particulars it mediates through instruments, and later there will come an elaboration of this. Transmitted evidences from the Book and the Sunnah have indeed indicated that the locus of originated knowledge is the heart, although it is not necessarily determined for that by reason; rather, it is permissible that God Almighty may create it in any substance He wills. However, the apparent view from the speech of many investigators is that what is meant by 'heart' is not that particular organ present in all animals, but rather the spirit by which man is distinguished. And the apparent speech of the philosophers is that the locus of knowledge of universals is the abstract rational soul, and of particulars is the external or internal senses, except that the investigators among them hold that the locus of all is the soul, but in universals it is essentially, and in particulars by mediation of instruments, namely the senses, and that will come later in the discussion of the soul.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage discusses the locus of knowledge. The author begins by stating that the locus is the heart, supported by transmitted evidence (السمع). Although God could create knowledge in any substance, the apparent meaning from revelation and many scholars is that 'heart' does not refer to the physical organ found in all animals, but rather to the spirit (الروح) which distinguishes humans. The author then contrasts this with the philosophers' view: they hold that the locus of knowledge of universals is the rational soul (النفس الناطقة), and of particulars is the senses. However, the more precise view among their investigators is that the soul is the locus for all knowledge, but in universals it is direct (بالذات), while in particulars it is through the mediation of the senses. The author notes that this will be detailed later in the discussion of the soul.

Correction: The author explicitly denies that the physical organ is meant; rather, 'heart' refers to the spirit (al-rūḥ) that distinguishes humans from animals.

Checkpoint

A) The physical heart
B) The rational soul
C) The heart (meaning the spirit)

Key Takeaway

The Intellect as the Locus of Obligation
Defines intellect and states its role in obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the intellect is according to the theologians and why it is the basis for religious obligations.

Core Idea
The intellect is the foundation of all religious responsibility.

Before we can discuss the details of Islamic obligations, we must first understand what makes a person accountable. The classical scholars defined intellect as both knowledge of necessary truths and the power to acquire theoretical knowledge. This lesson defines the intellect and establishes its role as the locus of taklif.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the section begins with a definition then moves to consensus.
The passage uses two different definitions; pay attention to the shift from 'the Shaykh said' to 'it is said'.
2
Identify the two definitions of intellect
The first definition is 'knowledge of some necessary matters.' The second is a power that enables acquisition of theoretical knowledge and moral discernment.
The second definition links intellect to both theoretical and practical reasoning.
3
Understand the consensus statement
The passage states there is no disagreement that the intellect is the locus of religious obligations. This means obligations only apply to those with intellect.
Children, the insane, and animals are exempt because they lack intellect.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the intellect is central to Islamic law. Without it, there is no accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع : العقل مناط التكليف
قال الشيخ : هو العلم ببعض الضروريات .
وقيل : القوة التي تحصل عند ذلك بحيث يتمكن بها من اكتساب النظريات، وهو معنى الغريزة التي يتبعها العلم بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات والقوة التى بها يميز بين الأمور الحسنة والقبيحة) .
لا خلاف فى أن مناط التكاليف الشرعية هو العقل حتى لا يتوجه على فاقديه من الصبيان والمجانين والبهائم، وسيجيء أن لفظ العقل مشترك بين
يمنع صاحبه
العقل في اللغة : هو الحجى والنهى وقد سمي بذلك تشبيهاً بعقل الناقة، لأنه من العدول عن سواء السبيل كما يمنع العقار الناقة من الشرود. والجمهور يطلق العقل على ثلاثة أوجه الأول: يرجع إلى وقار الإنسان وهيئة، ويكون حده أنه هيئة محمودة للإنسان في كلامه واختياره وحركاته وسكناته والثاني : يراد به ما يكتسبه الإنسان بالتجارب من الأحكام الكلية فيكون حده أنه معان مجتمعة في الذهن تكون مقدمات تستنبط بها الأغراض والمصالح والثالث يراد به صحة الفطرة الأولى في الإنسان فيكون حده أنه قوة تدرك صفات الأشياء من حسنها وقبحها وكمالها ونقصانها .
راجع معيار العلم للغزالي ص ١٦٢).
أما الفلاسفة فيطلقون العقل على المعاني الآتية : ١ - أول هذه المعاني قولهم : إن العقل جوهر بسیط مدرك للأشياء بحقائقها . (الكندي: رسالة في حدود الأشياء ورسومها).
وهذا الجوهر ليس مركباً من قوة قابلة لفساد ابن سينا: الإشارات ص (١٧٨)
The seventh section: The intellect is the locus of obligation. The Shaykh said: It is knowledge of some necessary matters. And it is said: The power that is acquired at that point, such that one can acquire theoretical matters through it, and it is the meaning of the innate disposition that is followed by knowledge of necessary matters when the faculties are sound, and the power by which one distinguishes between good and bad things. There is no disagreement that the locus of religious obligations is the intellect, so that obligation is not directed at those who lack it, such as children, the insane, and animals. It will come that the word 'intellect' is shared between... it prevents its possessor. The intellect in language: it is 'hija' and 'naha' (intelligence and forbearance), and it was named thus by analogy with the hobbling of the she-camel, because it is a turning away from the middle path, just as the hobble prevents the she-camel from straying. The majority apply the term 'intellect' in three ways: First: it refers to a person's dignity and demeanor, and its definition is that it is a praiseworthy demeanor in one's speech, choice, movements, and stillnesses. Second: it is used to mean what a person acquires through experience of universal judgments, so its definition is that it is meanings gathered in the mind that become premises from which purposes and benefits are deduced. Third: it is used to mean the soundness of the original innate disposition in man, so its definition is that it is a power that perceives the attributes of things, their goodness and ugliness, perfection and deficiency. See al-Ghazali's 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' p. 162. As for the philosophers, they apply the term 'intellect' to the following meanings: 1. The first of these meanings is their statement that the intellect is a simple substance that perceives things in their realities. (al-Kindi: Treatise on the Definitions of Things and Their Descriptions). And this substance is not composed of a power susceptible to corruption. (Ibn Sina: al-Isharat p. 178)
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. It defines the intellect (`al-`aql`) as the basis (`manat`) of religious obligation (`at-taklif`). It begins by citing the Shaykh's (likely al-Taftazani) definition: 'knowledge of some necessary matters' (`al-`ilm bi-ba`d ad-daruriyyat`). Then it gives another definition: the power that enables one to acquire theoretical knowledge and to distinguish good from bad. The author then states the consensus that the intellect is the locus of obligation, exempting those lacking it (children, insane, animals). The text then discusses the word 'intellect' as a homonym. It provides the linguistic origin: from `iqal al-naqa` (hobbling the she-camel), implying restraint. The majority (al-Jumhur) give three meanings: (1) a praiseworthy demeanor and dignity, (2) acquired universal judgments from experience, and (3) the sound innate disposition that perceives good and bad. Finally, the philosophers define it as a simple substance that perceives realities, incorruptible. This passage shows how theologians integrated linguistic, ethical, and metaphysical dimensions of intellect.

Key Terms
العقل
مناط التكليف
الضروريات
النظريات
الغريزة

D) It is limited to acquired theoretical knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The intellect, defined as knowledge of necessary truths and a power for theoretical and moral discernment, is the basis for religious responsibility.

The Multiple Meanings of Intellect
Classifies meanings of intellect: linguistic, majority's three, philosophical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origin of the term 'intellect' and the different meanings assigned by theologians and philosophers.

Core Idea
Intellect is a rich term with linguistic, ethical, and metaphysical dimensions.

The word 'intellect' (al-`aql) is used in multiple ways. In language it comes from hobbling a camel, implying restraint. The majority of scholars define it in three distinct senses: as dignified demeanor, as acquired universal judgments, and as innate moral perception. Philosophers add a metaphysical definition as a simple substance. This lesson unpacks these layers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and translation. Notice the passage moves from linguistic origin to the majority's classification, then to philosophical meaning.
Look for the phrase 'الجمهور يطلق العقل على ثلاثة أوجه' for the three meanings.
2
Understand the linguistic origin
The term 'aql' is derived from 'iqal' (hobble), implying restraint from straying. This shows that intellect is seen as a restraining force.
This is a common metaphor in Arabic.
3
Learn the three meanings of the majority
First: dignified demeanor. Second: acquired universal judgments. Third: innate moral perception. Each has a distinct definition and application.
These cover ethical, epistemological, and innate dimensions.
4
Consider the philosophical definition
Philosophers define intellect as a simple substance that perceives realities and is incorruptible. This is a metaphysical view.
This differs from the theological perspectives.

العقل في اللغة : هو الحجى والنهى وقد سمي بذلك تشبيهاً بعقل الناقة، لأنه من العدول عن سواء السبيل كما يمنع العقار الناقة من الشرود. والجمهور يطلق العقل على ثلاثة أوجه الأول: يرجع إلى وقار الإنسان وهيئة، ويكون حده أنه هيئة محمودة للإنسان في كلامه واختياره وحركاته وسكناته والثاني : يراد به ما يكتسبه الإنسان بالتجارب من الأحكام الكلية فيكون حده أنه معان مجتمعة في الذهن تكون مقدمات تستنبط بها الأغراض والمصالح والثالث يراد به صحة الفطرة الأولى في الإنسان فيكون حده أنه قوة تدرك صفات الأشياء من حسنها وقبحها وكمالها ونقصانها .
راجع معيار العلم للغزالي ص ١٦٢).
أما الفلاسفة فيطلقون العقل على المعاني الآتية : ١ - أول هذه المعاني قولهم : إن العقل جوهر بسیط مدرك للأشياء بحقائقها . (الكندي: رسالة في حدود الأشياء ورسومها).
وهذا الجوهر ليس مركباً من قوة قابلة لفساد ابن سينا: الإشارات ص (١٧٨)
The seventh section: The intellect is the locus of obligation. The Shaykh said: It is knowledge of some necessary matters. And it is said: The power that is acquired at that point, such that one can acquire theoretical matters through it, and it is the meaning of the innate disposition that is followed by knowledge of necessary matters when the faculties are sound, and the power by which one distinguishes between good and bad things. There is no disagreement that the locus of religious obligations is the intellect, so that obligation is not directed at those who lack it, such as children, the insane, and animals. It will come that the word 'intellect' is shared between... it prevents its possessor. The intellect in language: it is 'hija' and 'naha' (intelligence and forbearance), and it was named thus by analogy with the hobbling of the she-camel, because it is a turning away from the middle path, just as the hobble prevents the she-camel from straying. The majority apply the term 'intellect' in three ways: First: it refers to a person's dignity and demeanor, and its definition is that it is a praiseworthy demeanor in one's speech, choice, movements, and stillnesses. Second: it is used to mean what a person acquires through experience of universal judgments, so its definition is that it is meanings gathered in the mind that become premises from which purposes and benefits are deduced. Third: it is used to mean the soundness of the original innate disposition in man, so its definition is that it is a power that perceives the attributes of things, their goodness and ugliness, perfection and deficiency. See al-Ghazali's 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' p. 162. As for the philosophers, they apply the term 'intellect' to the following meanings: 1. The first of these meanings is their statement that the intellect is a simple substance that perceives things in their realities. (al-Kindi: Treatise on the Definitions of Things and Their Descriptions). And this substance is not composed of a power susceptible to corruption. (Ibn Sina: al-Isharat p. 178)
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. It defines the intellect (`al-`aql`) as the basis (`manat`) of religious obligation (`at-taklif`). It begins by citing the Shaykh's (likely al-Taftazani) definition: 'knowledge of some necessary matters' (`al-`ilm bi-ba`d ad-daruriyyat`). Then it gives another definition: the power that enables one to acquire theoretical knowledge and to distinguish good from bad. The author then states the consensus that the intellect is the locus of obligation, exempting those lacking it (children, insane, animals). The text then discusses the word 'intellect' as a homonym. It provides the linguistic origin: from `iqal al-naqa` (hobbling the she-camel), implying restraint. The majority (al-Jumhur) give three meanings: (1) a praiseworthy demeanor and dignity, (2) acquired universal judgments from experience, and (3) the sound innate disposition that perceives good and bad. Finally, the philosophers define it as a simple substance that perceives realities, incorruptible. This passage shows how theologians integrated linguistic, ethical, and metaphysical dimensions of intellect.

The term 'intellect' covers multiple layers: linguistic restraint, ethical demeanor, acquired wisdom, innate moral sense, and metaphysical substance.

Aql in Language and Common Usage
Al-Taftazani defines 'aql lexically and enumerates three common usages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical root of 'aql and the three common scholarly meanings: dignified demeanor, experiential wisdom, and innate perception.

Core Idea
The word 'aql derives from a camel's hobble and has three common meanings: demeanor, experience, and innate disposition.

Al-Taftazani begins the section on reason, which is the basis of moral obligation. He first explains the linguistic origin of 'aql and then outlines the three ways the term is used in common scholarship. This lesson unpacks those foundational definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the section title and the footnote. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Understand the lexical meaning
The footnote explains that 'aql in language means intelligence and forbearance, and is derived from the camel's hobble. Notice the analogy: just as the hobble prevents straying, reason prevents deviation.
This root metaphor is often overlooked.
3
Identify the three common aspects
The passage lists three aspects: 1) dignified demeanor, 2) acquired universal judgments from experience, and 3) sound innate disposition that perceive good and bad. Understand each definition and how they differ.
The first relates to external conduct, the second to acquired knowledge, the third to innate power.
4
Reflect on the progression
Notice how the definitions move from outward behavior to inner acquired knowledge to innate perception. This shows the breadth of the term in common usage.
Al-Taftazani references al-Ghazali for further reading.

The seventh section: reason (1) which is the basis of moral obligation. The majority use 'aql in three aspects: First: it refers to a person's dignity and demeanor, and its definition is that it is a praiseworthy demeanor in one's speech, choice, movements, and stillness. Commentary
This passage opens the seventh section on reason as the basis of moral obligation. It first presents the section title. Then, in a footnote, it provides comprehensive definitions of 'aql: lexically, it is derived from the camel's hobble because it prevents straying. In common usage, it has three aspects: (1) dignified conduct, (2) practical wisdom from experience, and (3) innate sound perception of good and bad. Philosophers define it as a simple substance that apprehends essences. After the footnote, the main text gives two scholarly definitions: one from the Sheikh (knowledge of some necessary truths) and another from others (the power that arises from that knowledge enabling acquisition of theoretical knowledge, which is the innate disposition, and the power to distinguish good from bad).

Aql in Philosophical and Technical Usage
Al-Taftazani gives the philosophical definition of 'aql as a simple substance and two technical scholarly definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of 'aql as a simple substance and the technical definitions of the Sheikh (knowledge of necessary truths) and others (a power for theoretical reasoning).

Core Idea
Philosophers define 'aql as a simple substance; scholars define it as knowledge or a power for reasoning.

Having covered the common usage of 'aql, al-Taftazani now presents the specialized definitions from philosophers and theologians. These definitions are more technical and emphasize the cognitive aspects of reason. This lesson examines the philosophical concept of 'aql as an incorruptible simple substance and the definitions attributed to the Sheikh and other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the two parts: the philosophical definitions and the two scholarly definitions. Compare them with the common definitions from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the philosophical definition
The philosophers define 'aql as a simple substance that apprehends things in their realities. This substance is not composed of a power susceptible to corruption. This is a metaphysical definition.
References to al-Kindi and Ibn Sina are given.
3
Examine the Sheikh's definition
The Sheikh (likely al-Tusi or another) defines 'aql as knowledge of some necessary truths. This ties 'aql to foundational knowledge that is self-evident.
4
Study the second technical definition
Another definition says 'aql is the power that arises from that knowledge, enabling the acquisition of theoretical knowledge. It is also equated with the innate disposition that precedes necessary truths and the power to distinguish good from bad.
This definition integrates the third common aspect.

Defining 'Aql as the Basis of Moral Obligation
Al-Taftazani establishes consensus and argues for defining 'aql as knowledge of some necessary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that reason is the basis of moral obligation and the specific definition of 'aql as knowledge of some necessary truths, supported by three conditional arguments.

Core Idea
Reason for moral obligation is the ability to acquire theoretical knowledge from self-evident basics.

This lesson establishes the foundational principle that reason ('aql) is the criterion for moral obligation (taklif) in Islamic theology. Al-Taftazani first states the consensus that legal obligations apply only to rational beings, excluding children, the insane, and animals. Then, recognizing the ambiguity of the term 'aql, he adopts the definition of the Sheikh: 'aql is knowledge of some self-evident universal truths that enable the acquisition of theoretical knowledge. He defends this definition through three conditional arguments, rejecting alternative interpretations. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the complete translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the structure: consensus, then definition, then three arguments.
2
Identify the consensus statement
Find the sentence that states that all scholars agree on the basis of obligations. Note the exceptions: children, insane, animals.
3
Understand the definition proposed
The author defines 'aql as knowledge of some necessary truths (self-evident universals). Understand what this means: basic innate knowledge that allows learning more.
This definition is chosen to avoid problems with other definitions.
4
Analyze the three conditional arguments
Break down each argument: (1) if not knowledge, separation absurd; (2) if theoretical, circular; (3) if all necessary, excludes valid cases. Ask why each is powerful.
These arguments show why the definition must be limited to some necessary truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خلاف فى أن مناط التكاليف الشرعية هو العقل حتى لا يتوجه على فاقديه من الصبيان والمجانين والبهائم، وسيجيء أن لفظ العقل مشترك بين معان كثيرة، فذهب الشيخ إلى أن المراد به هاهنا العلم ببعض الضروريات أي الكليات البديهية بحيث يتمكن من اكتساب النظريات إذ لو كان غير العلم لصح انفكاكهما بأن يوجد عالم لا يعقل وعاقل لا يعلم وهو باطل، ولو كان العلم بالنظريات وهو مشروط بالعقل لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه، ولو كان العلم بجميع الضروريات لما صدق على من يفقد بعضها لفقد شرطها من التفات أو تجربة أو تواتر، أو نحو ذلك مع أنه عاقل اتفاقاً .
There is no disagreement that the basis of legal obligations is reason, so that it is not directed at those who lack it, such as children, the insane, and animals. And it will come that the word 'aql is shared among many meanings. The Sheikh held that what is meant by it here is knowledge of some necessary truths, i.e., self-evident universals, such that one can acquire theoretical knowledge. For if it were other than knowledge, their separation would be possible, so that there could exist a knower who is not rational and a rational being who does not know, which is false. And if it were knowledge of theoretical matters, while it is conditioned by reason, the thing would be posterior to itself. And if it were knowledge of all necessary truths, it would not apply to one who lacks some of them due to the absence of their condition, such as attention, experience, or recurrent transmission, or the like, while he is rational by agreement.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It begins by stating the consensus that reason is the basis of legal obligations, thus obligations are not directed at children, the insane, and animals. Then it notes that the word 'aql is ambiguous. The author (Sheikh) specifies that here it means knowledge of some necessary truths, i.e., self-evident universals, that enable the acquisition of theoretical knowledge. He supports this with three conditional arguments: (1) If 'aql were not knowledge, they could separate, leading to a knower without rationality or a rational being without knowledge, which is false. (2) If 'aql were knowledge of theoretical matters, since theoretical knowledge itself requires 'aql, this would cause the thing to be prior to itself (circularity). (3) If 'aql were knowledge of all necessary truths, then someone who lacks some necessary truths (due to missing conditions like attention, experience, etc.) would not be considered rational, but he is considered rational by agreement. Thus, the conclusion is that 'aql must be knowledge of some necessary truths.

Key Terms
مناط التكليف
العلم بالضروريات
الكليات البديهية
النظريات
انفكاك
تأخر الشيء عن نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'aql includes all necessary knowledge or is identical to theoretical knowledge.
Correction: The author argues 'aql is only knowledge of some necessary truths that enable acquisition of theoretical knowledge, avoiding circularity and exclusion.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of the arguments against 'aql being knowledge of all necessary truths?
A) Some necessary truths require attention or experience, so a rational person may lack them
B) It would lead to infinite regress
C) It would make reason identical to knowledge
D) It would make theoretical knowledge impossible

Key Takeaway
'Aql is defined as the knowledge of some self-evident truths that enables the acquisition of theoretical knowledge, defended by three conditional arguments against alternative interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your intellect every day, but have you ever asked yourself: do I use my reason to seek the truth about my Creator, or do I let my desires and habits lead me? What necessary truths have you built your faith upon, and are they from self-evident realities or from blind conformity?
?
The author argues that reason is not just any faculty but a specific knowledge that enables further learning. If your intellect is meant to acquire theoretical knowledge about Allah and His commands, what are you actively doing to train your mind on those matters? What steps will you take this week to turn your 'aql toward its purpose?
?
If moral responsibility hinges on having this reason, then you will be asked about how you used it. Is your intellect a tool for truth or a defense for your own opinions? When you read a religious text, do you submit to its evidence or twist it to fit your preconceptions? Commit today to let your reason be guided by revelation, not rebellion.

Objection to the Proof of 'Aql and its Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding entailment in the proof from reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that distinct things can be inseparable is answered and why the counterexample of sleep does not refute the original argument.

Core Idea
Two distinct things can be inseparable, but that does not disprove the entailment.

The author's proof from reason faced an objection: what if two distinct things always accompany each other, like substance and accident? The objector also claimed that a rational being can exist without knowledge, as in sleep. This lesson presents that objection and shows why it is considered weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the concise style; the objection and answer are packed into one sentence.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage contains two moves: an objection (denying the entailment) and a secondary objection (denying the falsity of the consequent). The author dismisses both as weak.
3
Analyze the first objection
The objector argues that two distinct things can be inseparable, giving examples: substance with accident, cause with effect. Understand why this challenges the original proof.
The original proof likely assumed that if two things are distinct, they can be separated.
4
Analyze the second objection
The objector says a rational being can exist without knowledge, citing sleep. Think about whether this is a valid counterexample.
Consider the difference between actual knowledge and the power of knowledge.
5
Reflect on the refutation
The author calls the objections weak. Consider why: sleep does not remove the capacity for knowledge; the sleeper still has it potentially.
This shows the objector's examples do not truly break the entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بمنع الملازمة، فإن المتغايرين قد يتلازمان كالجوهر مع العرض والعلة مع المعلول وقد يمنع بطلان اللازم، فإن العاقل قد يكون بدون العلم، كما في النوم وهو ضعيف .
And it is objected by denying the entailment: for two distinct things may be inseparable, like substance with accident, and cause with effect. And it may be denied that the consequent is false, for a rational being may exist without knowledge, as in sleep. But it is weak.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objector denies the entailment in the author's first argument by pointing out that two distinct things can be inseparable, like substance and accident or cause and effect. The objector also denies that the consequent (a rational being without knowledge) is false, giving the example of a sleeping person who is rational but not actively knowing. The author dismisses this as weak, likely because sleep does not negate the possession of knowledge as a power; the sleeper still has the capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sleep proves that a rational being can exist without any knowledge at all.
Correction: In sleep, a person retains the power (or potential) for knowledge even if not actively knowing; the capacity remains, so the example does not show a rational being without knowledge simpliciter.

Checkpoint

B) An insane person
C) A child
D) An animal

Key Takeaway
Objections to theological proofs must be examined carefully; seemingly plausible counterexamples like sleep do not necessarily refute the original argument when the full context is considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson shows that even plausible objections can be weak. But how often do you treat your own doubts as automatically valid? What is one doubt you have been nursing that might actually be hollow? What step will you take to investigate it honestly?

The Preferred Definition of 'Aql as 'Aql bi-l-Malaka
Al-Taftazani gives the preferred definition of 'aql as a power arising from necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final preferred definition of 'aql as a power arising from necessary knowledge, enabling the acquisition of theoretical knowledge, and why it is called 'aql bi-l-malaka.

Core Idea
'Aql is a power, not a substance; it arises with necessary knowledge and enables theoretical discovery.

Al-Taftazani concludes his discussion of 'aql by presenting the most correct definition. He synthesizes views from Imam al-Haramayn, scholars of usul, and philosophers to arrive at a definition of 'aql as a power that arises from knowledge of necessary truths. This definition identifies 'aql as an acquired disposition ('aql bi-l-malaka) rather than an innate entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains the preferred definition and references to earlier views.
Notice the phrase 'والأقرب أن العقل قوة'.
2
Identify the three cited opinions
The passage references three different views: Imam al-Haramayn's, those who say it distinguishes good and bad, and usul scholars who call it a light.
Each is introduced by 'وما قال'.
3
Synthesize the definitions
Recognize that all three point to the same core idea: a power that arises from necessary knowledge and enables acquisition of theoretical knowledge.
This is the author's preferred synthesis.
4
Note the philosophical term
The passage ends by stating that philosophers call this 'aql bi-l-malaka.
This term is key for further study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقرب أن العقل قوة حاصلة عند العلم بالضروريات بحيث يتمكن بها من اكتساب النظريات، وهذا معنى ما قال الإمام إنها غريزة يتبعها العلم بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات، وما قال بعضهم إنها قوة بها يميز بين الأمور الحسنة والقبيحة وما قال بعض علماء الأصول إنها نور يضيء به طريق تبتدأ به من حيث ينتهي إليه درك الحواس، أي قوة حاصلة للنفس عند إدراك الجزئيات بها يتمكن من سلوك طريق اكتساب النظريات (١) وهو الذى يسميه الحكماء العقل بالملكة . (١) النظرية : قضية تثبت ببرهان وهي عند الفلاسفة تركيب عقلي مؤلف من تصورات منسقة، تهدف إلى ربط النتائج بالمباديء فإذا أطلقت النظرية على ما يقابل الممارسة العملية في مجال الواقع دلت على المعرفة الخالية من الغرض المتجردة من التطبيقات العملية وإذا أطلقت على ما يقابل العمل في المجال المعياري دلت على ما يتقوم به معنى الحق المحض أو الخير المثالي المتميز عن الالزامات التي يعترف بها الجمهور ونظرية المعرفة : هي التي تبحث في طبيعة المعرفة، وأصلها وقيمتها ووسائلها وحدودها . ونظرية النسبية : هي النظرية التي وضعها (آينشتين) على مرحلتين إحداهما مرحلة النسبية الخاصة عام ۱۹۰۵ والأخرى مرحلة النسبية العامة عام ۱۹۱۳ .
And the closest (view) is that 'aql is a power that arises upon knowledge of necessary truths, such that by it one can acquire theoretical knowledge. This is the meaning of what the Imam said: it is an innate disposition which is followed by knowledge of necessary truths when the faculties are sound. And it is what some said: a power by which one distinguishes between good and bad things. And it is what some scholars of usul said: a light by which the path is illuminated, starting from where the perception of the senses ends. That is, a power that arises in the soul upon the perception of particulars, by which it can follow the path of acquiring theoretical knowledge (1). And that is what the philosophers call 'aql bi-l-malaka. (1) Al-nazariyya: a proposition established by proof; according to the philosophers it is a mental composition of coordinated concepts aimed at linking results to principles. When nazariyya is used in contrast to practical practice in the realm of reality, it denotes knowledge free from purpose, abstracted from practical applications. And when used in contrast to action in the normative realm, it denotes what constitutes the meaning of pure truth or ideal good, distinguished from obligations recognized by the public. And epistemology (nazariyyat al-ma'rifa) is that which investigates the nature of knowledge, its origin, value, means, and limits. And the theory of relativity (nazariyyat al-nisbiyya) is the theory developed by Einstein in two stages: the special relativity stage in 1905 and the general relativity stage in 1913.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the final preferred definition of 'aql. It states that the closest view is that 'aql is a power that arises upon knowledge of necessary truths, enabling acquisition of theoretical knowledge. This is consistent with what Imam al-Haramayn said (an innate disposition followed by knowledge), and with those who say it is a power to distinguish good and bad, and with usul scholars who call it a light starting from where senses end. Essentially, it is a power that arises in the soul upon perception of particulars, enabling it to traverse the path of theoretical knowledge. This is what philosophers call 'aql bi-l-malaka. The footnote defines 'nazariyya' (theory) as a proposition established by proof, a mental composition linking results to principles. It distinguishes theoretical from practical knowledge, and mentions epistemology and the theory of relativity.

Key Terms
العقل بالملكة
الغريزة
النظريات
الجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Aql is a separate substance or identical to theoretical knowledge itself.
Correction: The text defines 'aql as a power that arises from necessary knowledge and enables acquisition of theoretical knowledge, not as a substance or the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
What term do the philosophers use for the kind of 'aql defined in this passage?
A) العقل الفعال
B) العقل بالملكة
C) العقل الهيولاني
D) العقل المستفاد

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of 'aql is that it is a power arising from necessary knowledge, enabling the acquisition of theoretical knowledge, and it is called 'aql bi-l-malaka.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If 'aql is a power that arises from necessary knowledge, then what are the necessary truths you hold most firmly? Do you actively use them as foundations for deeper understanding, or do you neglect them?
?
The text says 'aql enables distinguishing good from bad. When was the last time you relied on your intellect to discern right from wrong, and did you trust that discernment even when your desires pulled the other way?
?
Al-Taftazani describes 'aql as a light that illuminates a path beyond the senses. Are you living by that light, or are you content to be ruled by only what you can see and touch? What one step can you take today to exercise your 'aql to see what the senses cannot?

The Meaning of Nazariyya (Theoretical Knowledge)
Al-Taftazani defines nazariyya as a proposition established by proof and explains its uses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple meanings of nazariyya (theory) as defined by philosophers, its contrast with practice, and its application in epistemology and modern science.

Core Idea
Nazariyya is a proposition established by proof; it is a mental composition linking results to principles.

In a footnote, al-Taftazani provides a comprehensive definition of nazariyya (theoretical knowledge). He begins with the philosophers' definition as a proposition established by proof, then distinguishes theoretical from practical knowledge, and finally mentions two modern theories: epistemology and relativity. This lesson unpacks this rich definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic footnote
Read the footnote carefully. It defines nazariyya in philosophical terms and then gives specific senses.
Note the initial definition: 'النظرية : قضية تثبت ببرهان'.
2
Identify the three senses of nazariyya
The text contrasts nazariyya with practical practice, with action in the normative realm, and then gives examples.
Look for the phrases 'فإذا أطلقت النظرية على ما يقابل الممارسة العملية' and 'وإذا أطلقت على ما يقابل العمل'.
3
Understand the examples
Two examples are given: epistemology and the theory of relativity. These illustrate how nazariyya is used in specific fields.
This shows the term's contemporary relevance.

Definition of Intellect in Kalām
The author critiques definitions of 'aql and proposes a faculty-based definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over the definition of intellect in kalām and the preferred view that intellect is a faculty arising from knowledge of necessary truths.

Core Idea
Intellect is not mere reasoning; it is a faculty rooted in self-evident knowledge.

This passage explores various definitions of 'aql (intellect) and refutes alternatives. The author presents three arguments against defining intellect as simply knowledge of theoretical matters or all necessary truths, then responds to an objection, and finally gives the preferred definition: intellect is a faculty that arises from knowledge of necessary truths, enabling theoretical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for the three arguments the author uses against alternative definitions of intellect. Each argument is introduced with 'law' (if).
The arguments are: (1) if intellect were other than knowledge, they could separate; (2) if it were knowledge of theoretical matters, circularity; (3) if it were knowledge of all necessary truths, it would exclude some rational beings.
2
Understand the objection and its weakness
Notice the objection that denies the necessary concomitance and suggests sleep as a counterexample. The author dismisses this as weak. Reflect on why the objector's points do not undermine the main argument.
3
Grasp the preferred definition
The author's preferred view is that intellect is a faculty (quwwa) that arises from knowledge of necessary truths. This definition aligns with the philosophers' 'aql bi-l-malaka. Compare this with the other definitions mentioned.
The footnote at the end explains the term 'naẓariyya' (theoretical) in philosophical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معان كثيرة، فذهب الشيخ إلى أن المراد به هاهنا العلم ببعض الضروريات أي الكليات البديهية بحيث يتمكن من اكتساب النظريات إذ لو كان غير العلم لصح انفكاكهما بأن يوجد عالم لا يعقل وعاقل لا يعلم وهو باطل، ولو كان العلم بالنظريات وهو مشروط بالعقل لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه، ولو كان العلم بجميع الضروريات لما صدق على من يفقد بعضها لفقد شرطها من التفات أو تجربة أو تواتر، أو نحو ذلك مع أنه عاقل اتفاقاً .
واعترض بمنع الملازمة، فإن المتغايرين قد يتلازمان كالجوهر مع العرض والعلة مع المعلول وقد يمنع بطلان اللازم، فإن العاقل قد يكون بدون العلم، كما في النوم وهو ضعيف ، والأقرب أن العقل قوة حاصلة عند العلم بالضروريات بحيث يتمكن بها من اكتساب النظريات، وهذا معنى ما قال الإمام إنها غريزة يتبعها العلم بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات، وما قال بعضهم إنها قوة بها يميز بين الأمور الحسنة والقبيحة وما قال بعض علماء الأصول إنها نور يضيء به طريق تبتدأ به من حيث ينتهي إليه درك الحواس، أي قوة حاصلة للنفس عند إدراك الجزئيات بها يتمكن من سلوك طريق اكتساب النظريات (۱) وهو الذى يسميه الحكماء العقل بالملكة .
(۱) النظرية : قضية تثبت ببرهان وهي عند الفلاسفة تركيب عقلي مؤلف من تصورات منسقة، تهدف إلى ربط النتائج بالمباديء فإذا أطلقت النظرية على ما يقابل الممارسة العملية في مجال الواقع دلت على المعرفة الخالية من الغرض المتجردة من التطبيقات العملية وإذا أطلقت على ما يقابل العمل في المجال المعياري دلت على ما يتقوم به معنى الحق المحض أو الخير المثالي المتميز عن الالزامات التي يعترف بها الجمهور ونظرية المعرفة : هي التي تبحث في طبيعة المعرفة، وأصلها وقيمتها ووسائلها وحدودها . ونظرية النسبية : هي النظرية التي وضعها (آينشتين) على مرحلتين إحداهما مرحلة النسبية الخاصة عام ۱۹۰۵ والأخرى مرحلة النسبية العامة عام ۱۹۱۳ .
Many meanings: the Shaykh held that what is meant here by intellect is knowledge of some necessary truths, i.e., self-evident universals, such that one is able to acquire theoretical matters. For if it were other than knowledge, their separation would be possible—such that there would exist a knower who does not reason and a rational being who does not know—and that is false. And if it were knowledge of theoretical matters, while it is conditioned by intellect, then the thing would be posterior to itself. And if it were knowledge of all necessary truths, it would not apply to one who lacks some of them due to lacking their condition, such as attention, experience, or recurrent transmission, or the like, despite being rational by agreement.
An objection was raised by denying the concomitance: for two distinct things may be concomitant, like substance with accident and cause with effect. And [the objector] may deny the falsity of the consequent: for a rational being may be without knowledge, as in sleep—but this is weak. The more likely view is that intellect is a faculty that arises from knowledge of necessary truths, whereby one is able to acquire theoretical matters. This is the meaning of what the Imam said: that it is an instinct followed by knowledge of necessary truths when the instruments are sound; and what some said: that it is a faculty by which one distinguishes between good and bad actions; and what some scholars of uṣūl said: that it is a light by which the path is illuminated, starting from where sense perception ends. That is, a faculty that occurs to the soul upon perceiving particulars, by which one is able to follow the path of acquiring theoretical matters (1). This is what the philosophers call 'aql bi-l-malaka (intellect as a disposition).
(1) an-naẓariyya (theoretical proposition): a proposition established by proof. According to the philosophers, it is a mental composition of coordinated concepts, aiming to connect conclusions to principles. When 'theory' is used in contrast to practical application in the realm of reality, it denotes knowledge free from purpose, devoid of practical applications. When used in contrast to action in the normative realm, it denotes that which constitutes the meaning of pure truth or ideal good, distinguished from the obligations recognized by the public. Epistemology is the study of the nature, origin, value, means, and limits of knowledge. The theory of relativity is the theory formulated by Einstein in two stages: the special relativity stage in 1905 and the general relativity stage in 1913.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of 'aql (intellect) and its relationship to knowledge, particularly in the context of kalām. The author presents various opinions and refutes objections. The passage begins by stating that there are many meanings of intellect. The Shaykh (likely al-Ashʿarī or a commentator) holds that here 'aql means knowledge of some necessary truths (ḍarūriyyāt), i.e., self-evident universals (kulliyyāt badīhiyya), such that one is able to acquire theoretical matters (naẓariyyāt). The author then presents three arguments against alternative definitions:

1. If intellect were other than knowledge, it would be possible for them to separate—a knower without intellect or a rational being without knowledge—which is false.
2. If intellect were knowledge of theoretical matters, but it is itself a condition for theoretical knowledge, that would entail circularity (posteriority of a thing to itself).
3. If intellect were knowledge of all necessary truths, then someone who lacks some necessary truths due to missing a condition (like attention, experience, or tawātur) would not be considered rational, yet they are commonly agreed to be rational.

Next, the author mentions an objection (iʿtirāḍ) that denies the first argument's claim of necessary concomitance (mulāzama). The objector says two distinct things can be concomitant (e.g., substance and accident, cause and effect). Also, they might deny that the consequent is false: a rational being can be without knowledge during sleep. The author dismisses this objection as weak.

The author then gives the preferred view: intellect is a faculty (quwwa) that arises from knowledge of necessary truths, enabling the acquisition of theoretical matters. This aligns with what the Imām (likely al-Ghazālī) said: it is an instinct (gharīza) followed by knowledge of necessary truths when the instruments are sound. Others said it is a faculty for distinguishing good from bad; some uṣūlī scholars called it a light that illuminates the path starting from where sense perception ends. Essentially, it is a faculty acquired by the soul upon perceiving particulars, enabling the acquisition of theoretical knowledge—what philosophers call 'aql bi-l-malaka (intellect as disposition).

The passage also includes a lengthy footnote (1) defining 'naẓariyya' (theory) in philosophical terms, distinguishing it from practice and action, and giving examples like epistemology and relativity theory. This footnote is likely a later editorial addition.

The commentary follows the author's argumentative order: first the three refutations, then the objection and its weakness, then the preferred definition, and finally the footnote as an explanatory addition.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think intellect is simply the capacity to reason without any prior knowledge.
Correction: The passage argues that intellect specifically arises from knowledge of necessary truths; it is not a blank slate but a faculty built on self-evident knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what is intellect ('aql)?
A) Knowledge of all necessary truths without exception
B) A faculty that arises from knowledge of necessary truths, enabling acquisition of theoretical matters
C) The ability to distinguish good from bad without any prior knowledge
D) The possession of theoretical knowledge directly

Key Takeaway
Intellect is defined in kalām as a faculty that arises from knowledge of necessary truths, enabling the acquisition of theoretical knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your intellect, do you ever stop to examine the foundational self-evident truths it is built upon? What are some of those truths you have taken for granted today?
?
Considering that true intellect is a faculty arising from knowledge of necessary truths, in what areas of your life have you neglected to build that foundational knowledge? What step will you take this week to strengthen it?
?
If intellect is not mere reasoning but a gift that comes from recognizing clear truths, how does your pride in your own reasoning block you from humbly acknowledging what is self-evident about Allah and His creation?

Objection and Counter-Objection on Non-Identity
An objection and counter-objection regarding the necessity of the three cases for different things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the identity of willing and hating is defended against an objection about necessary concomitance, and how the defense shifts focus to awareness.

Core Idea
Willing a thing does not require awareness of its opposite, undermining the objection.

The previous argument asserted that if willing and hating were different, they must fall into one of three cases: similarity, opposition, or difference. An objector challenges the necessity of these cases, offering examples of things that are necessarily concomitant. The counter-objection then reframes the discussion by highlighting that willing occurs without awareness of the opposite. In this lesson, we analyze this back-and-forth to see how the identity thesis is upheld.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing deeply, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall dialectic.
Note the technical terms: تسليم, لزوم, متلازمين, ضدين.
2
Identify the objection
The objector denies the necessity that different things must be similar, opposite, or different. He gives two examples: things that are necessarily concomitant (e.g., fire and smoke) and things that are opposites of a single thing (e.g., black and white relative to grey).
The objector argues that if non-identity forces a case, combining two such things would force impossible coexistence of opposites.
3
Identify the counter-objection
The counter-objection points out that willing a thing often occurs without any awareness of its opposite. Even if there is awareness, there is no proof that willing entails hating, let alone identity.
This shifts the burden: the objector's scenario of necessary coexistence does not apply since willing and hating are not necessarily concomitant.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why the counter-objection succeeds. It undermines the objection by showing that the objector's assumed entailment between willing and hating is not present in ordinary experience.
This lesson shows how logical subtlety is used to defend central theological claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وورد بعدم تسليم لزوم أحد الأمور لأن المتخالفين قد يكونان متلازمين، أو ضدين لواحد، فلو لزم جواز اجتماع كل مع ضد الآخر لزم جواز اجتماع المتنافيين. وعورض بأنه قد يراد الشيء، ولا يشعر بضده، ثم على تقدير الشعور لا دليل على الاستلزام، وإن حكم به القاضي فضلاً عن الاتحاد.
And it was objected by not conceding the necessity of one of the [three] cases, because two different things may be necessarily concomitant or two opposites of one thing. So if it were necessary that each coincides with the opposite of the other, then the coincidence of the two opposites would be necessary. And it was countered that a thing may be willed without awareness of its opposite; then, on the assumption of awareness, there is no evidence for entailment, even if al-Qadi judged it, let alone identity.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a counter-objection. The objection is directed against the earlier argument that if willing and hating were different, they must be similar, opposite, or different. The objector says: non-identity does not force one of those three cases because two different things (mutakhālif) can be necessarily concomitant (mutalāzimān) – meaning whenever one exists the other exists – or they can be two opposites of a single thing (like black and white as opposites of grey? Actually, the objector gives the example of two things that are opposite of something else). So if each different thing were required to coexist with the opposite of the other, it could force two opposites to coexist, which is impossible. But the objector is questioning the premise that different things always allow such coexistence. The counter-objection shifts focus: willing a thing often occurs without any awareness of its opposite. Even if there is awareness, there is no proof that willing entails hating, let alone identity. This undermines the argument that willing must be identical to hating its opposite.

Key Terms

لزوم
متلازمين
ضدين
المتنافيين
شعور
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-identity always implies one of the three cases (similarity, opposition, difference).
Correction: The objection shows that two different things can be necessarily concomitant (mutalāzimān), so non-identity does not force a case. The counter-objection further shows that willing and hating are not necessarily concomitant because willing often occurs without awareness of the opposite.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the counter-objection to the first objection?
A) Different things cannot be concomitant.
B) Willing a thing does not require awareness of its opposite.
C) Al-Qadi's judgment is always correct.
D) Identity is proven by logical necessity.

Key Takeaway
The identity of willing and hating is defended by arguing that willing does not necessitate awareness or hatred of the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sincerely desire something for the sake of Allah, are you even aware of its opposite? For example, when you truly will to pray, do you consciously feel hatred for not praying at that moment, or is your will so pure that no awareness of the opposite crosses your mind? How does this passage expose the subtlety of your own intentions?
?
You often assume that because you are aware of a sin, you must hate it—but the counter-objection challenges this. Is your hatred of sin truly a separate act of the heart, or is it merely the absence of love? Tonight, before sleeping, examine one desire you have: do you hate its opposite, or are you simply unaware? What does that reveal about the state of your faith?
?
If willing does not require awareness of the opposite, then your will to obey Allah can be genuine even when you are not constantly fighting its opposite in your mind. Does this truth relieve you of the burden of perpetual inner conflict, or does it make you realize that your will is often scattered? Will you now train your heart to will single-mindedly, without needing to think about what you reject?
